Sec. 12. Thâ¬Â»jr departments, boards
commissioners find officers provided f.>
in this Charter? shall be entitled to th
possession of fill papers, books, docu
ments, maps, jilats, records and ar
es in the possession or under the
control of those respectively who are
superseded in office under this Charter
by such departments, boards, commis
sioners and officers.
Sec. 13. All books and records of
every office and department shall bo
open to the inspection of any citizen at
any time during business hours. Cer
tified copies or extracts from said books
and records shall be given by the offi
cer having the same in custody to any
person demanding the same, and pay
ing or tendering ten cents a folio of one
hundred words for such copies or ex
tracts; but the records of the Police
Department shall not be subject to
such Inspection except permission be
given by the Police Commissioners or
by the Chief of Police.
Sec. 14. The Treasurer shall keep his
office open for business every day, ex
cept legal holidays, from nine o'clock
in the forenoon until four o'clock in
the afternoon. Except where other
wise provided for by law, or by this
Charter, all other public offices shall be
kept open for business every day, ex
cept legal holidays, from half-past
eight o'clock in the forenoon until five
o'clock in the afternoon; and, in ad
dition thereto, from the first day of
November until the last Monday of De
cember in each year the office of the
Tax Collector shall "c kept open until
nine o'clock in the evening.
Sec. 15. No person shall be eligible to
or hold any office, or be clerk or dep
m.v in any office or department, who
has bf-on found guilty of malfeasance
in office, bribery or other infamous
crime or who in any capacity has em
beszh d public funds.
Sec 16. The fiscal year mentioned in
this Charter shall commence on the
first day of July and end on the thirti
eth day of June following.
Sec. 17. All moneys, assessments and
taxes belonging to or collected for the
use of the City and County, coming into
the hands of any officer of the City and
County, shall immediately be deposited
with the Treasurer for the benefit of
the funds to which they respectively
belong. If such officer for twenty-four
hours after receiving the same shall
delay or neglect to make such deposit,
he shall be deemed guilty of misconduct
in office and may be removed.
Sec. 18. Any elected officer, except
Supervisor, may be suspended by the
Mayor and removed by ihe Supervisors
for cause: and any appointed officer
may be removed by the Mayor for
cause. The Mayor shall appoint some
person to discharge the duties of the
office during the period of such sus-
Sec. 19. When the Mayor shall sus
pend any elected officer he shall im
mediately notify the Supervisors of
such suspension and the cause there
for. If the Board is not in session he
shall immediately call a session of the
Fame in such manner as shall be pro
vided by ordinance. The Mayor shall
present written charges against such
suspended officer to the Board and fur
nish a copy of the same to said officer,
who shall have the right to appear
with counsel before the Board in his
defense. If by an affirmative vote of
not less than fourteen members of the
Board of Supervisors, taken by ayes
and noes and entered on Its record, the
action of the Mayor is approved, thf>n
the suspended officer shall thereby be
removed from office; but if the action
of the Mayor is not so approved such
suspended officer shall be immediately
reinstated.
Sec. 20. When the Mayor shall re
move an appointed officer from office,
he shall immediately notify the Board
of Supervisors of such removal, and
furnish it a statement of, the cause
therefor, which statement shall be en
tered in the record of its proceedings.
Sec. 21. Unless otherwise provided
by law or by this Charter, any officer,
board or department authorized to ap
point any deputy, clerk, assistant or
employee, shall have the right to re
move any j erson so appointed.
Sec. 22. All appointments of officers,
deputies and clerks to be made under
any provision of this Charter must be
made in writing and in duplicate, au
thenticated by the person or person,
board or officer making the same. One
of such duplicates must be filed with
the Secretary of the Civil Service Com
mission and the other with the Auditor.
Sec. 23. Wherever it is provided in
this Charter that the members of any
board, department or commission shall
so classify themselves by lot that their
terms of office shall expire at different
times, such members shall, on the day
of making such classification, cause the
same to be entered in the records of
their proceedings, and a copy thereof,
certified by the Secretary thereof and
signed by all of said members, shall be
filed with the Clerk of the Supervisors.
In every case such classification must
be made at the first meeting of the
Seo. 24. Every officer and every mpm
ber of any board or committee provided
for in this Charter shall have the power
to administer oaths and affirmations,
and every such board, officer or com
mittee shall have power to issue subpe
nas, to compel by subpena the produc
tion of books, papers and documents,
and to take ami hear testimony concern-
Ing any matter or thins pending before
any such board, officer or committee.
If any person so subpenaed neglect or
refuse to appear, or to produce any
book, paper or document, as required
by such subpena, or shall refuse to
testify before any such board, officer or
committee, or to answer any question
which any officer or a majority of such
board or committee shall decide to be
proper or pertinent, he shall be
deemed in contempt, and any such
board, officer or committee shall have
power to take the proceedings in that
behalf provided by the general laws of
this State. The Chief of Police must.
on request of such officer or of any
member of any such board or com
mittee, detail a Police Officer or Officers
to serve such subpenas.
Sec. 25. All publications provided for
In this Charter must be made in the
official newspaper only.
Sec. 26. All franchises and privileges
heretofore granted by the City and
County which are not in actual use or
enjoyment, or which the grantees
thereof have not in good faith com
menced to exercise, are hereby de
clared forfeited and of no validity un-
Jess said grantees or their assigns shall,
within six months after tnis cnarter
takes effect, in good faith commence
the exercise and enjoyment of such
privilege or franchise.
Sec 9 7 All ordinances or resolutions
for the improvement of any street for
which no contract shall have been en
tered into at the time this charter
takes effect are hereby repealed
Sec. 28. All ordinances, orders and
resolutions of the Supervisors of the
City and County in force at the time
this Charter takes effect, and not in
consistent therewith, shall continue in
force until amended or repealed.

paid for a greater time than that

Charter shall be in full compensation
for all services rendered, and every of
ficer shall pay all moneys coming into
his hands as" such officer, no matter
from what source derived or received,
into the treasury of the City and
County within twenty-four hours af
ter receipt of the same.

partment shall require additional depu
ties, clerks or .employees, application
shall be made to the Mayor therefor.
and upon such application the Mayor
shall make investigation as to the ne
cessity for such additional assistance;
and if he find the same necessary he

ditional deputies, clerks or employees;
and thereupon the Supervisors, by an

teen members, may authorize such ap
pointments, and provide for the com
pensation of such appointees, subject to
the limitations contained in this Char
ter, and subject to the' provisions of
Article XIII thereof.

SCHEDULE.
This Chart - shall he published for
twenty days in The San Francisco Call
and in the Daily Report, daily news
papers of general circulation in the

after such publication, viz.: on Thurs

and ninety-eight, it shall be submitted j

the adopti"n of the same; and if a ma
jority of the qualified electors of said

mltted to ih .- Legislature of the State
of California for its approval or rejec
tion. If the Legislature shall approve
the same, it shall take effect and be in
force, except as hereinafter otherwise
provided, on and after the hour of noon
on the first Monday after the first day
of January in the year nineteen hun
dred, and shall tb reupon become the!
Charter and organic Law of the City i
and County of San Francisco, and shall
Bupersec^ the existing Charter of said:
City and County, and all amendments

this Charter.

A FATAL BLOW.
Otto Senn Killed Accidentally by a
Fellow Workman at the Union
Iron Works.
Otto Senn, of 137 Perry street, a ma
chinist employed in the Union Iron
Works, died in the Waldeck Hospital yes
terday fn^ii the effects of a blow with
tamer wielded by Thomas John
son, a i> How workman, on April 11. Senn
am! the workman were engaged on a
steel shaft driving Imlts. The hammer
slipped off the head of the bolt and struck
him over the pit of the stomach inflicting
injuries which caused the death of Senn.
Senn was taken to the Waldeck Hospi
tal, where an examination disclosed the
hat his stomach w;is ruptured and
that there was do hope [ >r his recovery.
The deceased told his wife shortly before
his death that the blow was accidental
and that he could not blame any one.
The body was taken to the Morgue, and
an inquest was waived by the relatives
of the deceased. Hence no inquest will
be held.
Poolrooms Decreasing.
According to the reports of the mem
bera of the Merchants' Association the
fight of that body against the poolrooms
is already productive of good results.
Many of the sharpers have already closed
their shops, and the Chief of Police has
promisfd to see that they do not open
some other kind of "skin game" in the
city. It is the belief that before very
long not a single poolroom will be left
open.

EXPLANATION.
The arrow flies with the wind. The top flg
1 ures at station indicate minimum temperature
for the days: those undernenth it. If any, t'ne
amount of rainfall or of melted snow In Inches
and hundredths during the . ast twelve hours.
Isobars, or solid lines, connect points of equal
air pressure; isotherms, or dotted lines, equal
temperature. The word "high" means high
barometric pressure and is usually accompanied
by fair weather; "low" refers to low pres-
Fure and Is Usually preceded and accompanied
by rloudy weather and rains. "Lows" usually
first appear on the Washington ?oast. When
the pressure Is hish In the interior and low ,
along the coast, and the isobars extend north
and south along the coast, rain ia probable; I
but wlien the "low" is inclosed with Jso'iars of 1
marked curvature, rain south of Oregon is <m- j
probable. With a "high" in the vic.nlty of !
Idaho, and the pressure falling to the Cali
fornia coast, warmer weather may be expected
in summer and colder weather in winter. The
reverse of these conditions will produce an
opposite result.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, April 15.â Professional traders
bought stock this morning, causing an advance
in prices of a point or over in the speculative
favorites, but most of these takings were sold
again before the expiration of the day's ses
sion, carrying prices back over their own
course and leaving net changes in almost all
cases Insignificant, but with small gains pre
dominating. The party on the exchange which
espoused the bull side yesterday found cause
for encouragement in the advance In the price
of Americans on the London exchange this
morning. Other European Bourses also showed
a firm tendency for Americans, and Spanish 4s
rallied from an early depression before the
opening of trading here. The cause of this
strength was a rumor that the powers had
Intervened between the United States and
Spain and that the Cuban insurgents had
agreed to an armistice. This rumor seemed
to have great force in foreign circles and
effected a sympathetic rise here at the open
ing, which was assisted by the professional
manipulation. Reports from London indicate
that there has been extensive dealing in ou
tions on Americans in that market and to-day s
demand was in large part from the sellers of
these options for the purpose of covering their
contracts. Whatever credence attached to the
intervention theory abroad, it was not deemed
important in this country, and the buying move
ment was quickly exhausted. The midday
period of Intense dullness and sagging prices
gave way to- a more active selling movement in
the final hour on belief that the Senate would
take action before adjourning. The definite
information from London that Great Britain
would refuse to Join in a naval demonstration
in behalf of Spain, and the Austrian Embadsa
dor'B open avowal that he had given up hone
of Europe preventing war. was notÂ» received in
Wall street until after the close. Neither was
the news that the regular army had been or
dered to the seaboard. Dealings were on a
small scale and the wide movement of prices
indicated a small floating supply of stocks
holders maintaining their waiting attitude. The
money market continued firm to-day and the
demand from the interior continued unabated
The supply of currency has become so scarce
that some of the city banks are shipping gold
In response to the interior demand. Further en
gagements of nearly $1,500,000 in gold for im
port were made to-day, notwithstanding the
hardness of the London money market and the
disinclination of Paris or Berlin to supply gold.
The Paris exchange on London as a. result
Jumped l'/4 centimes to 25 francs 36 centimes,

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, April 15.â Trade In wheat started
â with the usual jump in May and new high
prices were made for that delivery. May
closed yesterday at Jl 11H. but opened to-day
at all the way from $1 12 to $1 14, and in two
minutes' time it had been bid up to $1 15. Not
since 189? has wheat reached such a high
level. A sharp break to $1 10 followed, fol
lowed by a quick rally to Jl 11%. After that
all interest near the close was centered in
the avalanche. July opened at S?%@B7%c, com
pared with yesterday's closing price of 87% c.
Hundreds had It for sale at those figures, and
the decline which started did not halt until
86% c was recorded. There the market rested,
but only for a few minutes. Liquidation was
redoubled and it was estimated that at least
5, 000,000 bushels was dumped on the market
in the hour- and a half which followed the
opening, chiefly for Letter's account, and the
heavy lines he disposed of, coupled with his
enormous cash sales, the last week must
have materially reduced his line of holdings.
The Liverpool market was %@%d lower before
this market opened, but apart from that no
realization for the weakness could be gath
ered from thÂ« news. The heavy holders were
evidently destined to realize their profits on
the r>-oent large advance, and their action
brought nearly all the small "tallers" in witti
their lines of long wheat. After the July price
had (rot down to S6V4c the market attain
quieted down to a certain extent, and up to
12:30 the decline was very gradual. But in the
last half hour's trading liquidation was re
sumed again and this time May also suffered,
though more from lack of support than from
an extensive offering. It was evident then
that the early bulge in May was part of a
cleverly prearranged plan to help Leiter li
quidate his July holdings, and It undoubtedly
did act as a steadying: Influence during the
n>3t tremendous fiord of wheat. The market
becam2 very weak toward the close and the
lowest prices of the day for July were quoted.

after selling at $1 08.

BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, April 15.â The Commercial Bulletin
â will say to-morrow of the Wool market: All
records for dull business are broken as far as
domestic Wools are concerned. Beyond sample
bags thpre has been no business except some
small lots of scoured yarns growth fine Texas
sold by an outside 6i>eculator at 46c. The
sales of foreign are swollen by the shipment of
p;ut of a wrecked cargo, 458 bales of Cape
Wool, in bond .to Canada. All credits are now
most carefully scanned, especially since the
utterly unexpected assignment of the felt works
at Dolgeville has succeeded the partially an
ticipated failure of Sawyer & Manning. The
grfat worsted mills have begun to shut down
machinery.

DUN'S REVIEW* OF TRADE.
TJEW YORK, April 15.-R. O. r>un & Co.'a
Weekly Review of Trade to-morrow will say:
The meaning of Congress regarding Spain is
not in doubt, but the form of declaration at
this hour is in question. Preparations for war
continue as if it were inevitable. There Is
some hindrance In business and yet the vol
ume of payments through clearing houses is
still 12.9 per cent larger than last year, and
outside of New York about 2.2 per cent larger
than in 1892, though speculative stagnation
here makes the aggregate lower. New York
banks may now realize that they are strong
enough for all possible emergencies and may
give the Government valuable aid by greater
freedom in business loans. Sound New York
banks are not in the least alarmed about tha
business situation, nor have they any occa
sion for alarm. p
The industries are doing well, because they
have enormous orders taken In February and
March or earlier, which Insure operations for
periods varying from a fortnight to several
months. But the new orders, upon which they
have to rely if partial stoppage or closing of
many works is to be prevented, are lust now
restricted by apprehension about the money
market and the possibility of loans. In the
Iron business there are orders for about 20 000
tons of structural work held up by questions
of law; in Chicago the large postofflce order
is yet waiting, and the Russian order for
rails is still pending, possibly because of finan
cial difficulties. While new business in this
line is still very heavy, the production Is so
large that It may easily be disastrously check
ed by a persistent restriction of the current
demand. But foreign orders for six large ves
sels are pending In Eastern shipyards, and
actual orders for cars alone cover 20 000 tons
basic steel at Pittsburg. Angles have ad
vanced $1 per ton and structural works are all
crowded with orders, while pipes are in heavy
demand.
With prices for print cloths still at the low
est point ever touched, buying: of cotton goods
Is more hindered than many are willing to
admit by the idea that other goods, not equal
ly overproduced, may likewise decline In price.
The stagnation In wool is something remarka
ble, the total sales at the three chief mar
kets having been only 1,182,144 pounds, against
9, 61*. 600 last year, and in the first half of
April only 3.385,044 pounds, against 22,346.000
pounds a year ago. Manufacturers are still
waiting, although somewhat encouraged by a
rather better tone in the clothing trade of late.
They have ample orders for the present in most
lines, though not in all. Wheat rose o 1 * cents
and held 2V2 cents for the week, although At
lantic exports for the week have been 3,045.021
bushels (flour Included), against 57fi,719 bush
els last year, and Pacific exports 827,36* bush
els, against 212,056 last year. The total from
both coasts for two weeks of April has been 7,
379.136 bushels, against 3.057. 452 bushels last
year. The receipts from Western farms show
some decrease for the week, but for two weeks
are only about 600, 000 bushels smaller than a
year ago. Meanwhile, it Is astonishing that
receipts of corn continue remarkably heavy,
for the week more than 1.000.000. larger than
last year, and for two weeks more than 1.
650,000 bushels, and the exports for the week
have been 4,653,031 bushels, against 2.705.73S
bushels last year and for half of April 5, 146,681,
against 6.032,015 bushels last year. Such ex
traordinary exports of corn go lar to show that
the outward movement of wheat is by no
means of a speculative character, nor likely
to diminish speedily. The official report shows
that exports In March were 15.459.575 bushels
of wheat (flour included), against 7.901.722
bushels last year, and In nine months from
the principal ports, 163,909,730 bushels, against
132,676,463 last year Meanwhile, the exports
of corn In the nl;ie months have been 135.905,
934 bushels, against 136.634,231 last year.
The volume of legitimate business Is enor
mous. Meanwhile failures are remarkably
small for the week: there have been 2:>4 in the
United States against 207 last year, and 18 in
Canada, against 30 last year.

BRADSTREET'S REVIEW.
NEW YORK, April 13.â Bradstreet's to-mor
row will say: The effect of the prevailing un
certainty in foreign affairs Is reflected in a
further perceptible quieting down of general
trade, so far as future engagements are con
cerned, ir. nearly all parts of the country.
While there is a little more activity to be
noted In the demand for seasonable staples at
the West and Northwest, where better weather
has helped retail and wholesale trade alike,
rather more quiet is reported at the East and
at the South Atlantic and Gulf ports, where
the Interruption to business has become par
ticularly serious, owing to the foreign con
troversy. The least notable effects yet ex
hibited are found In the Central West, where
distributive demand and movement are report
ed of a seasonable character, the only changes
njted, in fact, being the strengthening of
money rates and the discouragement of new
enterprises, such as railroad building. In the
Iron trade business continues of large vol
ume, but the bulk of this Is claimed to be on
aciount of orders previously booked, and new
business, where noted, is for small individual
lots. Price weakness Js more marked also than
for some time past. A feature of the week is
reported to be the gradual getting together of
the non-Bessemer Iron purchasers, who follow
ing the example set by other branches of that
business, are said to have formed a combine
which will include nearly all the manufactur
ers of foundry plgiron between the Alleghanies
and the Rocky Mountains. Wool continues dull
at nominal prices at the East, and the activ
ity in dry goods is confined chiefly to goods
needed at once. Cotton goods prices are steady
except for iTlnt cloths and talk of curtailing
produ"lton Is continued. Low-price woolens
continue in most active demand in this branch
of trade. A heavy export demand is reported
ami prices aro considerably higher. The price
situaticn is one of comparative steadiness
Foreign imnrÂ»f sions as to the situation in
Cuba are redacted in sharp advances in wheat,
flour, corn and lard and other food products
Declines are to be seen In Bessemer pig-iron
steel billets, coffee, sugar and lead, while as
compared with a week ngo, the price of oats,
pork, beef, molasses, copper, cotton, wool and
the manufactured products of both of those
staples are generally steady and unchanged
The business failures in the United States
for the week number 215, against 220 last week
195 In the corresponding week of 1597. 244 In
1806 and 219 in 1895.
Exports of cereals are larger. The shipments
of wheat (Hour Included) this week from the
United States and Canada aggregate 4,044 000
bunhels, against 3,778,000 bushels last week,
1.344,000 bushels In this week a year ago, 2,017 -
000 bushels In 1596, 3.165,000 bushels in 1895 and
3,019,000 bushels In 1594.
REVIEW OF THE STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. April 15.â Bradstrwt's, of the
New York stock market to-day, to-morrow w..i
say: While the public continues to watch the
stock market intently it takes no active part
in the proceedings. The professionals have ap
parently arrived at the conclusion that the un
favorable elements in the situation have been
discounted and that prices are more likely to
advance than to decline, no matter what the
outcome of the country's relations with Spain
may be. Stocks have passed largely into strong
hands and the floating supply of them is small
The short interest Is very much reduced

Jacob Wollner's circular says:
"The wool market, both here and In the East,
is Just the same as it has been for the last
three or four months. There Is no wool sell
ing and no inquiry. Usually during the preva
lence of a dull sjriell there Is some looking
around for weak spots or wools in weak hands
that try to effect sales by offering below the
market, but such Is not the case, however, at
present. Although there is no market, prices
are not shaded and cannot be quoted lower
than they were at the beginning of the year.
As soon as the war scare is settled we can
look for some business. There have been no
sales at all in this market anil Â»-->w wool*
which arrived <iuite freely on consignment are
not offered, as there is no use in owning them
if there are no buyers.
"Some of the Interior newspapers claim. that
the San Francisco wool merchants have com
bined and agreed not to buy any wools In the
country. so that the growers would have to
ship their wools to this market on consignment.
If we had a market or any demand at all
there would be lots of buyers in the country,
but as most of the wool dealers have .large
stocks on hand from last year they do not
feel like loading up with new wool when they
cannot sell It at present. Just as soon as
business is revived there Is no doubt but that
we will have plenty of buyers."

Hanlson's circular says:
"The following coal deliveries have been
made here this week, viz. five from British
Columbia, with 17.33s tons; two from Wash
ington, 6400 tons; two from Oregon, 1030 tons;
two from Australia. 4Sf>9 tons; total. 29.627 tons.
The arrivals are about equal to our consump
tion, hence prices remain about the same and
stocks In yards are undisturbed. Jobbers re
port trade dull; profits are fair, but orders are
not coming in freely. Some of our factories
are liberal fuel consumers, filling orders which
have been created by the anticipated needs of
our Government in case of war being declared.
This has made values steady for steam grades.
But few contracts are being made for future
deliveries of foreign coal, as the margins of
profit are cut so fine that the importers can
not cover their sales if a war insurance rate
is added. Coal is an article contraband of
war. hence liable to confiscation If the carrier
of same should be overtaken by Spanish gun
boats or privateers, if destined for an Amer
ican port, besides being a munition of war
which the Spaniards moet need. It Is most
singular that Australian and .English coal
freights have not been materially advanced in
view of the dismal outlook for grain carriers
to receive a moderate compensation for their
services on arrival here. If they continue to
come it is an undeniable fact that the carry-
Ing trade of the world Is at a very low ebb.
and other ports offer no better inducements
than we do.

KCTVIâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of ths
day in the oruer of nccurrence as to time. ThÂ«
second time column gives the second tide of
the flay, the third time column the third tide,
and the last or right hand column gives ths
last tide of the day, except when there art but
three tide*, as sometimes occurs. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height
â nd then the number given Is subtractlve from
the depth given by the charts. The plans of
reference Is the mean of tt lower low waters.
NOTICE TO MARI..ER3.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlo
Office, located In the Merchants" Exchange, Is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
2122
mariners ' without regard to nationality and
!ree of expense.
Navigators are cot tally Invited to visit tbÂ»
Jfftce. where complete sets or charts and salr-
Ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matter*
Of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time hall on top of the building on Tele
graph Hill Is hoisted about ten minutes befor*
noon and Is dropped at noon. 120 th meridian,
by telegraphic signal received each day from
(lie United States Naval Observatory at Mart
Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the time ball was
dropped on time â  r giving the error. If any, la
published the same day by the afternoon
papers, and by the mornlne papers the follow
ng day. W. S. Ht'GHFS.
Lieutenant. V. S. N.. in charge.

JORDAN INDICTED.
"United States Grand Jury Presented
a True Bill Against the Post
office Janitor.
The United States Grand Jury yester
day presented an Indictment In the United
States District Court against John W.
Jordan "for embezzling from the United
States mall a valuable letter" on the sth
of April of this year. .
Jordan is a colored man and had been
employc-d for about two years as porter
and janitor in the general delivery de
partment of the postofflce in the Custom
House building. For several weeks mail
from the interior addressed to the gen
eral delivery had been missing, together
with money inclosed in the letters. Pos
tal Inspectors Munro and Erwin set a
watch on Jordan and detected him in the
ii< t of abstracting from a letter silver
certificates to the value of $3.
Being caueht in the act Jordan con
fessed that he had been stealing money
from letters for two months. The let
ters he destroyed after opening, by burn
ing them in the stove in the general de
livery department. The prisoner will be
arraigned in Judi?e de Haven's court this
morning and will, no doubt, plead guilty,
as he is being prosecuted on only one
charge.

San Francisco Too Attractive

At the Session of the State Central

of the sights, of which San Francisco has
enough and to spare. This character
istic Is thte most pointed argument the
outside counties can bring against the
city as a place for the meeting of the
Democratic convention during the coming
fall.

GREGGAINS CAN NOW SLEEP.
The Referee Has Solved a Problem That Has Been Keeping
Him Awake.
A NEW DEPARTURE IN THE PRIZE RING,
Alec Greggains can now sleep at night. He has been troubled with Insomnia since the day that It was decided
that he should referee the coming mill between Sharkey and Jeffries, not that he had any doubt as to his ability to do
Justice to the case, but through an instinctive disposition to preserve himself from possible contact with the fists of
either of the big fellows who will enter the ring for the purpose of demolishing something;.
It is well understood among sporting men that when Jeffries and Sharkey get together trouble will brew for the
man who tries to make them break away when they shall have clinched, and just how the referee should be protected
from accidental violence was a question that has called forth more thought than the arrangements for the mill. This
has been the cause of the nervous trepidation on the part of Greggains. and the solution of the problem is .the cause
of the superabundance of smiles tha,t now illuminate the genial face of the referee.
It was well known that the face would be the vulnerable point should the enthusiasm of the pugilists get beyond
control, and it has been finally decided that Billy Woods' pneumatic face, such as was used at Carson, would afford
necessary protection in that respect. So far all was well, but Alec was bitterly opposed to having his arms and legs
used as possible fulcrums or his body becoming a mass of battered flesh when he attempted to separate the clinching
rivals for fistic honors.
One happy afternoon Greggains strolled out to the park In order to think In the wide free air, and an Inspira
tion took him to the museum. As he wandered aimlessly about his eyes chanced to fall upon a suit of antique armor
and the whole question was solved. Negotiations were entered into and the coat of mail was leased with privilege of
such alterations as might be found necessary, and now Alec is happy. A blacksmith friend was called Into requisi
tion and additional spikes welded on the steel frame with corresponding stiffening of the joints. While Greggains
would hardly be called an available candidate for a beauty show in his new apparel, he feels that much can be sac
rificed in appearance when safety of life and limb are in consideration, and the result is a combination that will di
vide the honors with the big fellows In the ring.
Sharkey and Jeffries have been shown the combination suit that will be worn by the referee, and they have be
come duly Impressed with the fact that if they fail to break away at command they are liable to fall afoul of some
thing worse than a knock-out blow, hence it is believed by those who have been "put on" that this fight will be one
of the most unique in the annals of local fistiana.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, APRIL 16, 1898.

The hotel proprietors have also pledged
themselves to furnish accommodations for
the delegates at reasonable rates.
JAPS TO BE DEPORTED.
Commissioner Rodgers of Philadel
phia Compliments North on
His Methods.
The work of the Special Board of In
quiry In the matter of seventy-one Jap
anese who arrived on the steamer Peru
was concluded yesterday afternoon. The
board recommended that five of the num
ber be returned by the steamship com
pany to Japan, it being %;lear that they
had come to this country under slave la
bor contracts. The five were the gang,
one of whose number was caught by Im-
migration Inspector Geffeney tearing up
a labor contract, and who tried to settle
the matter by offering the Inspector $100.
Several others were detained for fur
ther investigation, one member of the
board having declined to sign the report
admitting them to this country.
United States Immigration Commis
sioner Rodgers of Philadelphia, who was
sont hither from Washington to investi
gate the truth of the complaint made by
the Japanese Minister at Washington that
his countrymen were being discriminated
against, left for home last night. Before
his departure he highly complimented
Commissioner North on the efficiency of
his office and the thoroughness anl im
partiality of his methods. He will re-
port that the facts have been misrepre
sented to the Japanese Minister.
Commissioner Rice of Victoria, B. C,
who had come to this city for the pur
pose of familiarizing himself with the
methods pursued by Commissioner North,
will leave for his post this morning. He
and Mr. North have agreed upon a uni
form procedure in the cases of all Im
migrants of all nationalities coming un
der their Jurisdiction.
ACCIDENTALLY ASPHYXIATED.
Thomas Wilkinson Came to His
Death Because of a Leaky Gas
Tube in His Boom.
If Thomas Wilkinson, tailor, 130 Hayea
street, hart been a regular reader of the
newspapers his body would not now be
lying on a slab in the Morgue.
Wilkinson was found dead in his bed
at 8:30 o'clock yesterday morning: the
room was full of the fumes of illuminat
ing gas. Deputy Coroner O'Brien made
an investigation and found that the dead
ly gas had escaped from a rubber tube
connecting the gas fixtures with a stove
used for heating: tailors' -Irons. The de
ceased, on retiring, had turned on" the
gas at the stove, and the tube being leaky
had allowed the gas from the fixtures to
fill the room. Had he turned the stop
cock at the fixtures and thus prevented
the gas from going into the tube the ac
cident would not have happened.
The deceased was a native of England,
aged about 38 years, and was divorced
from his wife some time ago. He pos
sessed some shares of stock in the Conti
nental Building and Loan Association and
some money in the Pacific StateÃ Savings
and Loan Association, the whole amount
ing to $500.

with dark eyes, and dark hair cut abort.
LOOK OUT FOR
YOUR CHIMNEYS
The March Earthquake
Held Responsible for
Recent Fires.
Fire Marshal Towe Gives
Property-Owners Some
Timely Advice.
American Biscuit Factory and Golden
Gate Villa Fires Referred to
as Illustrations.
The poor little earthquake of March 30
that caused so much excitement In the
city Is still having bricks thrown at It.
Fire Marshal Towe says that It is re
sponsible for something more than scar
ing people out of their wits and break
ing a lot of windows, and he thinks that
before next winter is over it will cause
the loss of many thousands of dollars.
The day following the earthquake the
American Biscuit Company's factory on
Broadway and Font streets was totally
destroyed by fire. The fire was attrib
uted to the earthquake by cracking the
top of the oven. The flames were first
observed by the watchman at the top of
the oven, which was about two feet from
the floor above,
Thursday nighfs fire at the Golden
Gate Villa, Ocean Beach, is also at
tributed to a crack in the chimney caus
ed by the earthquake. When the flames
were first sÂ«-en they were round the kit
chen chimney in the vicinity of the
crack. The kitchen fire was being used
at the time to cook tamales.
.. 1 T h , av c not the slightest doubt," said
the iMre Marshal yesterday, "that both
of these tires were due to the earth
ly V and there wlir be a lot more
through the same cause before next win
ter is over. The flames get in the cracks
an Â« >n time the woodwork catches fire
and it is a difficult matter to extinguish
the lire before considerable damage is
done.
"I would advise owners of buildings to
ov f rh u ul the chimneys for cracks and
get them repaired, otherwise they may
suffer serious loss. People are not using
many v Â» f ln thls weather, otherwise
more buildings would have suffered, but
when the cold weather comes then I ex
pect there will be trouble. I don't want
to alarm people unnecessarily, but it
would be well for property owners to
take my advice."
To be forewarned is to be forearmed,
Â£ ih t advlcc Â°f the Fire Marshal
should be acted upon. It may cost a lit
tle money but it may be the means of
saving valuable property from destruc
tion and probably save some lives.
A CASE OF THOMAS.
The Kate Thomas in the Hands of the
Thomases for Luck and Has a
Thomas Cat Mascot.
A case of Thomas, like history repeat
ing itself, came to light in the Custom
house yesterday. The ship Kate Thomas
cleared for Queenstown, Ireland. Her
captain is William Thomas, and she is
owned by William Thomas & Co.. who
are not of any kin to the captain. The
captain's sister is Kate Thomas. The
ship was entered at this port by Customs
Broker W. Ford Thomas, no relative of
any of the other Thomases, and he also
has a daughter named Kate Thomas.
The ship was entered in the name of
Customs Broker Harry B. Thomas, and
took away with her a large Thomas cat
as an additional mascot.
INCONVENIENCES HXJNTINGTON
He Wants the Bailroad Commission
ers to Hear Him at an Earlier
Date.
The Board of Railroad Commissioners
were in receipt of the following com
munication yesterday from C. P. Hunt-
Ington, president of the Southern Pa
cific, which explains itself:
_ April H. 1898.
To the Honorable Board of Railroad Com
missioners of the State of California-Gentle
men: I am in receipt of your notification to
me to appear before your board on the ilth
day nf May. at 1 o'clock. In the matter of the
establishment by your board of rates oi
of charges for the transportation of passen
gers and freight by the Southern Pacific Com
pany, and I beg to say. with all deference to
your board, that while I shall be quite ready
and glad to meet your honorable body and
state all information in my power concerning
the railroads with the control and direction
of which I have the honor to be connected.
I ask your courtesy In fixing upon an earlier
date for my appearance before you. My busi
ness duties are pressing and important and I
am likely to be called away from San Fran
cisco at almost any time after a week more
shall have elapsed. I reel quite confident that
a compliance with my request cannot Incon
venience the board nearly as much as my at
tention to your notice is certain to Incon
venience me, and hence I a.=k your favorable
consideration of it. Very truly,
C. P. HUNTINGTON.
Those of the Commissioners who are
out of the city will be notified of the re
ceipt of the letter by the board, and the
wish therein expressed by President
Huntington, that they permit him to 'ap
pear before them at an earlier date. In
all probability the Commissioners will
grant the request of the railroad mag
nate.
CADETS VOLUNTEER.
A Well-Drilled Regiment of Young
Men Offer Their Services to
the Governor.
Among the first organized bodies to
proffer their services to the Government
in case there should be war is the First
Regiment of the League of the Cross
Cadets of this city. The following letter
was sent by their commander to Gov
ernor Budd to-day:
SAN FRANCISCO, April 15, 1898.
Hon. James H. Budd, Governor of California.
Sacramento, Cal.â Sir: At a meeting: of the
board of officers of the First Regiment,
League of the Cross Cadets, held last even
ing, I was instructed by a unanimous vote to
place at your command the services of the
regiment for any duty that may be neces
sary tv maintain and defend the nation's honor.
The regiment is well drilled and disciplined
and is composed of 3 battalions; two of five
companies each and one of lour companies;
hospital corps, band and the requisite field,
staff and non-commissioned staff officers; in
all a total volunteer force at this writing of
706. I am preparing roster showing name, age
and height, which will be forwarded within the
next few days. I trust that this application
will receive your favorable consideration. Very
respectfully, W. P. SULLIVAN JR.,
Colonel Commanding First Regiment, L. C. C.
BIG MEN BEGIN
THEIR TRAINING
Sharkey at Work in Vallejo
and Jeffries Preparing
in Oakland.
Baker and Stelzner to Be Matched
for the Preliminary Bout of
the Evening.
Tom Sharkey, the patriotic marine, has
postponed his intention to enlist and fight
the Spaniards until after his go with Jim
Jeffries. The Sailor has already begun
training for the big event, and has Henry
Baker to assist him. As Thomas always
tires two men out when down to work,
he made overtures to Jack Stelzner to
join his camp. Stelzner might have done
so but for the fact that Jeffries had al
ready gobbled him up. Joe Kennedy will
now assist the marine.
Sharkey will train in Vallejo up to with
in a week or two of the fight, when he will
go to the Ocean Beach and finish up.
His old training quarters were destroyed
by fire on Thursday night, and Tom will
have to engage others.
Lon Agnew, the clever lightweight,
thinks Jeffries will prove the victor in
the coming go. As Agnew has trained
with Choynski, sparred with Jeffries, and
likewise with the Sailor; his opinion is
entitled to some consideration. Agnew
thinks Jeffries the strongest man in the
ring to-day, and as he has a good left
and is a good judge of distance, the Sail
or's rushes will not avail him.
As in the past Jim will train at the Re
liance club rooms under the watchful eye
of Billy Delaney.
Dal Hawkins left for New York on the
overland yesterday with the hope of be
ing pitted against Daly. Since his victory
over Eddie Connolly, Dal has become con
fident that he can take Lavigne's meas
ure.
It is said that Joe Goddard, the Barrier
champion, will try his fate again by en
tering the ring against Peter Maher. A
good offer has boen made him by the
Arena Club of Philadelphia for a go be
tween the Australian and the big Celt for
May 6 or 13. Joe, like Barkis, "was
wlllin'," and on receipt of the offer re
plied that the 6th would suit him, but
wanted more particulars before journey
ing East.
Sacramento Athlot.? Club has matched
Young Griffo and â¢'Bull" McCarthy to fight
twenty rounds for a decision on the 27th
of this month. The men will fiÂ£rht at
140 pounds, and Alec Greggains has been
chosen referee. McCarthy has met Owen
Zeigler and other leading lights, and has
made a fine showing against them, and
as Griffo is taking care of himself a good
bout should result.
Lon Agnew was in town yesterday look
ing better than he has in many months.
The ligaments in his right shoulder, which
he strained in one of his bouts, are in
their normal condition, and he is able to
use his right with as great force as in
former contests. Agnew says he would
rather meet "Spider" Kelly than any man
in the business. He claims Kelly was
given a decision over him by Sharkey
without cause. He thinks that Kelly
would be an easy mark for him, and
would like to retrieve himself.
The Manhattan Athletic Cluo will hold
another of its popular monthly boxing ex
hibitions on the evening of the 29th. The
best available material in the city has
been selected for the occasion and some
interesting goes will result.
Managers Groom and Gibbs, of the Na
tional Club, expect to have a good ap
petizer for the big go, by matching Baker
and Stelzner. As both men are in rival
camps training the big men, a fight be
tween them would attract nearly as much
Interest.
